    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b19a229c635843d783c2e82aed01ec2a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ecce1306-1413-429c-955b-18bdddac1bc2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d8242362" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Stabilizing the electrode interphases is urgently required to enhance the lifetime of high-voltage sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the continuous anode solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) growth associated with electron leakage and the fragile cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) lead to capacity fade at high voltage; and yet the solvation-interphase-performance relationship is inadequately addressed. Herein, a cooperative Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation strategy is reported to stabilize the interphases by a holistic design of electrolytes combining soft and moderate co-solvents. The rationally regulated Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation leads to CEI/SEI with the desired thickness and component stability. As such, remarkable cycling stability is achieved for 4.3-V Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F (NVOPF) cathodes with 83.3% capacity retention over 3000 cycles at 1 C, significantly outperforming the carbonate counterpart (41.6% capacity retention). Meanwhile, the restrained SEI growth via reducing the formation of electron-leaking Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> stabilizes the long-term cycling of the hard carbon (HC) anode. The assembled NVOPF||HC full cells achieve superior rate capability (up to 15 C) and stable cycling stability over 500 cycles. The demonstrated engineering of electrolyte chemistry, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation, and interphase structure/component contributes toward the rational establishment of design rules for high-voltage SIBs and possibly other similar chemistries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403138-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with the merits of sustainability and low cost are highly desired in the static energy storage sectors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0001_R_d8242329e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Sci. Adv. Adv. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Of particular significance, efforts to develop high-energy-density SIBs are desired to compete with the current lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-voltage operation of the batteries offers high capacity, voltage, and thus high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, despite the great achievements made in high-voltage cathodes, stable cycling of SIBs is shadowed by the rapid growth and yet severe dissolution of the solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI), the damage of cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) and structure degradation of cathodes in conventional carbonate electrolytes at high voltages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> An ideal SEI/CEI has to be ion-conductive but electron-insulating. It is acknowledged that the electrolyte, as the paramount component for constructing the robust SEI/CEI, ultimately determines the overall performance such as cycle life, Coulombic efficiency (CE), and the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, achieving stable electrode interphases via electrolyte engineering holds the key to developing practical high-energy SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Suppression of unstable interphase growth, including CEI and SEI, is highly required for achieving long-term cell cycling stability. Upon cycling, the reduction products from ethylene carbonate-based electrolytes such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precipitate on the hard carbon (HC) anode surface as part of the SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-rich SEI is vulnerable to electron leakage due to the low unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) level and narrow bandgap from Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0005_R_d8242329e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, there is continued SEI growth to a thickness that is required to block the electron leakage, which leads to rapid electrolyte depletion, irreversible capacity loss, and short cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0006_R_d8242329e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed a high-concentration (3.04&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) ether-based electrolyte featuring strong Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation and inhibited solvent activity which successfully enabled 4.3-V operation of the cell. In addition, Zhang et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0007_R_d8242329e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported a localized highly concentrated electrolyte (LHCE), in which an anion-derived SEI rich in fluoride was formed, offering desired durability, reversibility, and safety. A high concentration of fluorine in SEI may however cause a large steric hindrance hindering the formation of organic sodium compounds that offer superior ion-migration kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0008_R_d8242329e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Phys. Chem. C ACS Energy Lett.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, restraining continuous SEI growth by minimizing Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> formation while regulating fluoride compositions is important for the long-term cycling stability of hard carbon (HC) anodes.</p>
         
         <p>Nevertheless, addressing the challenges on the anode alone often failed to satisfy the harsh requirements of the high-voltage cathodes. Anodic decomposition of conventional carbonate solvents (e.g., ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC)) forms an unstable and partially soluble CEI on the cathode, resulting in continuous electrolyte decomposition and fast capacity fade<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0005_R_d8242329e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0009_R_d8242329e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>; this is particularly aggravated in the case of high-voltage batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0009_R_d8242329e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0010_R_d8242329e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome the challenge, a low-concentration carbonate electrolyte (0.3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC:PC:FEC; FEC: fluoroethylene carbonate) generated a dense and uniform CEI and afforded a favorable capacity of 109.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) cathode at −25&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0011_R_d8242329e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Afterward, such electrolyte was further reported to enable NVPF with a fast-charging capability even at 100 C under 55&nbsp;°C owning to the anion-derived CEI. Nevertheless, it remains an open challenge to demonstrate long-cycling-life and high-voltage SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0012_R_d8242329e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The solvation-interphase-performance relationship for SIB electrolytes is not yet as well-established as that for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0013_R_d8242329e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Mater. Nat. Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the knowledge gained from LIBs cannot be directly transferred to the SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0014_R_d8242329e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast to the carbonate- or ether-based electrolytes that have shown limitations in either thermal/electrochemical stability, ionic conductivity or poor wettability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0015_R_d8242329e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> succinonitrile (SN) as an electrolyte solvent demonstrates advantages such as high stability against oxidation, strong salt-dissociating capability, high thermal stability (boiling point: 266&nbsp;°C) and nonflammability, which have been validated for high-voltage LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0016_R_d8242329e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, its poor reduction stability against alkali metals leads to its continuous decomposition along with uncontrolled SEI growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0016_R_d8242329e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0017_R_d8242329e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nature of SN having a high dielectric constant (<i>ε</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;54) may further facilitate dissolution of SEI if it comprises of substantial amount of soluble components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0016_R_d8242329e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such issues may have hindered the study of SN in SIBs because in general the Na<sup>+</sup> compounds, whether inorganic or organic, as observed in the SEI dissolve more readily than their Li<sup>+</sup>-counterparts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simply using additives such as FEC to regulate fluoride formation in SEI has shown some success,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0018_R_d8242329e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Electrochim. Acta">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but not effectively eliminated the interphase degradation. The cell performance in harsh conditions (i.e., high-voltage and wide-temperature operation) largely relies on stable interphases via rational electrolyte design; yet, few studies on electrolytes have been devoted to simultaneously restraining SEI growth and constructing robust CEI.</p>
         
         <p>To fill the gap in revealing the solvation-interphase-performance relationship and further regulate interphase components for high-voltage SIBs, we propose a strategy that relies on regulating the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation via the design of cooperatively solvating solvents. A low-freezing-point and moderately solvating carboxylic ester (methyl acetate (MA) or ethyl acetate (EA)) and the oxidation-stable SN are used as dual solvents, and their participation in the first Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation sheath mainly determines the preferential decomposition of species at the electrode-electrolyte interfaces. Central to the solvent selection is the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvating power (donor number, DN) and dielectric constant (<i>ε</i>) to simultaneously tame the CEI and SEI with beneficial components and suppress their extensive growth/dissolution. Consequently, the co-solvated electrolyte leads to a robust CEI rich in F-/N-inorganics and an SEI rich in organic compounds but poor in Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, rendering high compatibility with the high-voltage NVOPF cathode and HC anode. Long cycling life with capacity retention of 83.3% over 3000 cycles at 1 C is achieved for 4.3-V NVOPF cathode based on the electrolyte of 1&nbsp;mol sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) in a dual-solvent of M:SN (1:1 by volume, M = MA, EA) with 5% FEC. Further, the simultaneously regulated SEI and CEI enable NVOPF||HC full cells with stable cycling over 500 cycles at 1 C.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403138-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202403138-sec-0030-title">2.1 Solvation Structure Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Carboxylic esters generally show a weaker solvating capability but meanwhile lower freezing points than the carbonates; therefore, we propose herein to couple carboxylic esters with SN, a molecule with high intrinsic oxidation stability. Despite different molecular chain lengths, both MA and EA show obvious merits to facilitating low-temperature operation of SIBs owing to their lower viscosity (0.37 and 0.45 mPa·s), freezing point (−98 and −84&nbsp;°C) and appropriate dielectric constant (<i>ε</i><sub>MA</sub> = 6.67; <i>ε</i><sub>EA</sub> = 6.0) compared to conventional carbonate and ether solvents (see Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0019_R_d8242329e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the narrow electrochemical window hinders their application for high-voltage SIBs, and on the other hand, the poor reduction stability of SN has prevented it from being a main electrolyte solvent. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1a–c</a>, we hypothesize that the formulation of low-temperature carboxylic ester and oxidation-stable SN dual solvents to co-solvate the Na<sup>+</sup> could be mutually reinforcing and resolve this dilemma. As a demonstration, we investigated electrolytes prepared with 1&nbsp; mol NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in the main solvents of SN and MA or EA with 5% FEC (denoted as MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC); and a benchmark carbonate electrolyte was prepared with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in EC: PC (1:1 by volume, denoted as EC-PC) as a comparison.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403138-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e7e01261-45ae-46f0-a204-620ed30a92bc/adfm202403138-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e7e01261-45ae-46f0-a204-620ed30a92bc/adfm202403138-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e5514539-8863-4857-aafe-8ce37f5babd5/adfm202403138-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e7e01261-45ae-46f0-a204-620ed30a92bc/adfm202403138-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403138-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403138" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Co-operative electrolyte design principle based on the co-solvation of SN and carboxylic ester for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries. a) Schematic of the molecular strategy used for the co-solvated electrolytes; DN: donor number. Generally, DN can be used to assess the coordination capability of solvents to Na<sup>+</sup>, while the dielectric constant (<i>ε</i>) is used to assess the solvent's polarity. b) The representative solvating clusters between Na<sup>+</sup> and the solvent ligands in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes as observed by molecular dynamics (the snapshots are found in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). c) A scheme to show the effect of co-solvating clusters on the formation of favorable electrode interphases. d) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra showing the weaker Na<sup>+</sup> solvation in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes compared to the EC-PC electrolyte. e) Radial distribution functions and the corresponding coordination numbers (N(r)) around the Na<sup>+</sup> in the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes, were extracted from MD simulations. f) Electrostatic potential (ESP) maps of the representative solvation clusters formed in EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes, color scheme: N (blue); O (red); C (gray); Na (purple); Cl (green); H (white); F (yellow). g) Calculated LUMO and HOMO energy levels of various solvents and the representative solvation clusters in different electrolytes. Insets are their corresponding molecular structures and energy orbitals.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We first conducted <sup>23</sup>Na nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) and Raman spectroscopy to reveal the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation structure in the electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra show a weaker solvation capability for Na<sup>+</sup> in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC compared to that in EC-PC, as evidenced by the downfield shift (more positive) of <sup>23</sup>Na chemical shift (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0020_R_d8242329e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, an emerging Raman peak is observed at 2264&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC which can be attributed to the coordination of C≡N···Na<sup>+</sup>, implying participation of SN in solvating Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0021_R_d8242329e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The free C≡N band of SN shifts from 2256&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> to a lower wavenumber of 2254&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating an obvious intermolecular interaction occurs upon mixing MA or EA (low freezing point of &lt; −80&nbsp;°C) with SN (high melting point of &gt; 50&nbsp;°C), which is a result of the N─H hydrogen bonds between them.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0019_R_d8242329e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0022_R_d8242329e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Cryst. Growth">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the formulation accounts for the higher ionic conductivity of the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes in the temperature range of −25–60&nbsp;°C compared to the carbonate benchmark (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>We performed molecular dynamics (MD) simulations to visualize the solvation structures in the electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1b–g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Radial distribution functions (RDFs) show that MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC in the first solvation sheath present two main distinct peaks with different bonding lengths, i.e., Na-O (at 2.4&nbsp;Å) and Na–N (at 2.5&nbsp;Å) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1e</a>). This confirms the participation of MA or EA molecules in the solvation structure along with SN (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1b</a>). Besides, the higher coordination numbers of Na─O bonding than Na─N bonding in both MA-SN-FEC (<i>N</i><sub>(r, Na–O)</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.6; <i>N</i><sub>(r, Na–N)</sub> = 0.9) and EA-SN-FEC (<i>N</i><sub>(r, Na–O)</sub> = 2.7; <i>N</i><sub>(r, Na–N)</sub> = 1.0) implies SN shows a comparatively weaker Na<sup>+</sup>-solvating ability than EA/MA,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0023_R_d8242329e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Mater. Devices">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> despite its higher dielectric constant (<i>ε</i><sub>SN</sub> = 54; <i>ε</i><sub>MA</sub> = 6; <i>ε</i><sub>EA</sub> = 6),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0016_R_d8242329e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0019_R_d8242329e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating a donor number dictated solvation scheme herein. Note that the coordination number of Na–O (FEC) or Na–F (FEC) is much lower than Na–O (MA or EA) and Na–N (SN) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information), again indicating that the MA/EA and SN are the dominant molecules in the first Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation sheath. We further calculated the electrostatic potential (ESP) maps for the involved solvents in order to reveal possible binding sites for Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1f</a>). Specifically, the minimum electrostatic potentials of solvent molecules all lie on the O atoms for EC, PC, EA, and MA, while SN bears negative potentials on N with obviously less electron density (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The strong binding between cyclic carbonate esters (EC and PC) and Na<sup>+</sup> may impede the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and charge exchange.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0024_R_d8242329e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, combining more weakly solvating linear carboxylic esters with SN can reduce the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation energy, as evidenced by the decreased electron density in the solvation clusters from MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1f</a>). The decreased Na<sup>+</sup> solvation capability and the co-solvation scheme in the designed electrolytes are in alignment with the experimental results from NMR and Raman measurements.</p>
            
            <p>In order to reveal which components of the electrolytes (i.e., solvent molecules, additive or representative Na<sup>+</sup> clusters) are preferentially decomposed at the interfaces, we performed quantum chemistry calculations to analyze the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and LUMO energy levels of various components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1g</a>). Notably, the Na<sup>+</sup>-EC-PC-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> complex within the EC-PC electrolyte may preferentially decompose on both anode and cathode sides as it shows a lower LUMO and a higher HOMO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1g</a>), suggesting an EC- and PC-derived SEI/CEI. In contrast, a higher HOMO energy level is observed in the solvating clusters (e.g., −6.82&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-(MA-SN-FEC-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>; −7.24&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-(EA-SN-FEC)-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) other than those free solvent molecules (e.g., MA: −7.33&nbsp;eV; EA: −7.26&nbsp;eV; SN: −9.14&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0024_R_d8242329e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating the preferential oxidation of solvating clusters on cathode surface and thus leading to a SN-derived CEI. Meanwhile, the lower LUMO levels of these solvating clusters than the solvent molecules also indicate their preferential reduction on the anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0024_R_d8242329e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the solvation-interphase correlation can be established herein to guide electrolyte design by complementing the respective advantages of SN and carboxylic ester (MA or EA). Indeed, the linear scan voltammetry (LSV) measurement confirms that both our designed electrolytes provide high durability against oxidation up to 4.8&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information), surpassing the performance of the EC-PC electrolyte. The thermal stability and viscosity of the electrolytes are present in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e,f</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202403138-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>We further demonstrate the cycling stability of high-voltage half cells in our electrolytes with an NVOPF cathode, which exhibits a high specific capacity of ≈126.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and discharge voltage plateau at ≈4.0&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). For the cell in EC-PC electrolyte, the rapid capacity fades with a sharp increase in cell polarization between the 1<sup>st</sup> and the 3000<sup>th</sup> cycles were observed, meaning a significant energy loss (the grey-colored area) in the cell (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2a</a>). Also, the corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> profiles show rather undefined redox peaks after 3000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2b</a>), demonstrating sluggish electrochemical kinetics. As a result, only 41.6% capacity retention with low average CE (98.8%) was retained over 3000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, with MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes, the cells showed much less energy loss in the charge–discharge profiles over 3000 cycles and maintained well-defined redox profiles as shown by the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> profiles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2a,b</a>), indicating superior electrochemical reversibility of the cells. Further, the cells showed high capacity retention of 98.9% (93.5%) over 1000 cycles and 83.4% (82.3%) over 3000 cycles (≈210 days) with an average CE of 99.4% (99.4%) at 1 C in the MA-SN-FEC (EA-SN-FEC) electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). When cycled at a higher current density of 2 C, the cells using the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes exhibit excellent cycling performance with a capacity retention of ≈85.1% (84.7%) and an average CE of 99.5% (99.6%) over 3000 cycles, outperforming the one using EC-PC electrolyte (44.5% and 98.8%; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403138-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/12436129-86cc-4531-b400-a5bc8d14f8d5/adfm202403138-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/12436129-86cc-4531-b400-a5bc8d14f8d5/adfm202403138-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cb1cbacf-f84e-4832-a028-3b1ff6ddbfb9/adfm202403138-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/12436129-86cc-4531-b400-a5bc8d14f8d5/adfm202403138-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403138-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403138" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparison of long-term cycling performance of NVOPF half cells using the three electrolytes at ambient temperature and a low temperature of −25&nbsp;°C. a,b) Voltage profiles of the first and 3000<sup>th</sup> cycle and the corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> profiles of the NVOPF half cells using EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC, and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes. c,d) Long-term cycling performance of the NVOPF half cells at 1 C and 2 C at 25&nbsp;°C after three conditioning cycles at 0.1 C. e) The cycling performance of NVOPF half cells at −25&nbsp;°C showing the high delivered capacity over 125 cycles at 0.1 C in the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes. The percentage in (c–e) is referenced to the capacity retention. The unexpected increase in capacity (c–e) stems from unplanned temperature increases inside the testing chamber.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Besides, we have conducted the experiments by testing cells using EC-PC electrolytes with 0.5% FEC and 5% FEC included for comparison. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the cell with EC-PC-0.5% FEC demonstrated less capacity fade than the cell with EC-PC, but exhibits a lower capacity than the designed electrolytes. This is perhaps due to the formation of a thick SEI layer for the sodium metal counter with EC-PC-0.5% FEC. However, the cell with EC-PC-0.5% FEC shows a lower capacity and a faster capacity fade at both current densities of 1 C and 2 C compared to those in our designed electrolytes of MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC. This is also true for the cell with a higher fraction of FEC, i.e., EC-PC-5% FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, we further selected another solvent tris (2,2,2-trifluoroethyl) phosphate (TFP) as the main solvent for a comparison. The cells with TFP-SN-FEC electrolytes show stable cycling at both 1 C and 2 C, but the capacity is lower compared to the electrolytes with MA (EA) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). These results imply that using only FEC additive cannot effectively eliminate the interphase degradation, and the main solvent MA (EA) and SN in the designed electrolytes could promote the high-voltage cell performance.</p>
            
            <p>Further, the cell performance was also evaluated at a lower temperature of −25&nbsp;°C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2e</a>, the cells with MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes delivered a higher capacity (≈104.8 and ≈106.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) than the one with EC-PC (≈75.6&nbsp;mA h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and showed high cycling stability. Such high capacity at −25&nbsp;°C can be attributed to the robust CEI, as discussed below. In addition, the carboxylic ester propyl propionate (PP) was also selected to co-operatively solvate with SN for a demonstration. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), stable cycling stability was obtained by using PP-SN-FEC electrolyte at both room temperature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a>, Supporting Information) and low temperature (−25&nbsp;°C, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information), again indicating the function of carboxylic ester molecules in boosting the cell performance.</p>
            
            <p>We also demonstrate the high compatibility of MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC with the hard carbon (HC) anodes over long-term cycling. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3a</a> shows that the HC half cells using the two designed electrolytes delivered gradually increasing capacity over cycling and an average CE up to 99.9% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information) for both MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC at a current of 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Further, among the three electrolytes, a high capacity of 280&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained in the HC cell with EA-SN-FEC electrolyte at a current density of 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the cell with EC-PC electrolyte shows continuous capacity decay over cycles at 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and lower capacity retention of &lt;12% over 2500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3a</a>). The capacity increase during the first 1000 cycles in the designed EA- and MA-based electrolytes was likely due to the gradually stabilized interphase at the sodium metal counter electrode and hence reduced cells’ overall overpotential over cycling, which allows the HC electrode to exhibit higher capacity within the voltage range of operation (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information for more details). Indeed, by examining the voltage profiles, we see decreased overall overpotential in the EA- and MA-based electrolytes compared to that in the EC-PC electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403138-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e19ba935-78b4-423a-8e54-8c95c860b4b8/adfm202403138-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e19ba935-78b4-423a-8e54-8c95c860b4b8/adfm202403138-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae4bf16b-4a98-41c6-a051-b2a249ba797a/adfm202403138-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e19ba935-78b4-423a-8e54-8c95c860b4b8/adfm202403138-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403138-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403138" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of HC half cells and NVOPF||HC full cells using EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC, and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes. a) Long-term cycling stability of the HC half cells at 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Initial three cycles of voltage profiles of the HC half cells with different electrolytes at a current density of 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Schematic of NVOPF||HC full cells. d) The cycling stability of the NVOPF||HC full cells at 1&nbsp;C. e) Rate performance of the NVOPF ||HC full cells and subsequent cycling at 0.5 C. The C-rate of the full cells with an N/P ratio of ≈1.3 was calculated based on the mass of NVOPF. The unexpected increase in capacity (a,d,e) stems from unplanned temperature increases inside the testing chamber.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, for demonstration of the effect on interphase regulation on both electrodes, NVOPF||HC full cells were fabricated using these three electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3c</a>). At a rate of 1 C, stable cycling with high capacity retention of 82.8% (73.5%) was achieved for the NVOPF||HC full cells using EA-SN-FEC (MA-SN-FEC) electrolyte, superior to the cell using EC-PC electrolyte (52.8%; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3d</a>). In addition, a slightly reduced voltage polarization was observed for NVOPF||HC full cells with MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes compared to the one with EC-PC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Also, the full cells with the MA- or EA- based electrolyte show superior rate capability with higher capacity at all current densities of 0.1–15 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0003">3e</a>). We further optimized the conditions (i.e., N/P ratio = 1.1; cut-off voltage: 1.4–4.3&nbsp;V; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), and the full cells with MA-SN-FEC, EA-SN-FEC and EC-PC electrolytes deliver a higher initial capacity of ≈125.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with lower voltage polarization at 0.1 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a–c</a>, Supporting Information) compared with those in the N/P ratio of 1.3 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Also, a stable cycle is obtained in cells with MA-SN-FEC in contrast to that with EC-PC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the NVOPF||HC full cells with EC-PC deliver a higher capacity than that in EC-PC-5% FEC and PC-5% FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The reason for this observation is that the FEC in the EC- and PC-based electrolytes may cause the thick CEI/SEI layer and thus suppress the capacity. Also, we assembled the NVOPF||HC full cells with the MA-SN electrolyte (FEC-free) for a comparison, which shows stable capacity over the first three cycles, further confirming the important role of the main solvents of MA (EA) and SN in stabilizing SIBs (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information for more details). Overall, the above electrochemical results lead to evidences on stabilized interphases at the two electrodes enabled by the designed electrolytes, which are highly correlated with the long-term cell operation.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202403138-sec-0050-title">2.3 Analysis of the SEI Interphase</h3>
            
            <p>We first characterized the components and morphology of SEI at the HC anode to reveal its dependence on Na<sup>+</sup>-solvation by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM). The XPS measurements were performed on the HC anodes after 250 cycles at 150&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). It is found that Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is largely present throughout the Ar<sup>+</sup>-sputtered thickness in EC-PC electrolyte, confirming its predominance in both the inner and outer SEI (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4a–e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0025_R_d8242329e1215" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the gradual increase in total Na atom and decrease in total C atom with increasing sputtering depth indicate a further increased fraction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> for the inner SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4a</a>). Besides, the quantified atomic ratios of the elements in EC-PC show high ratios for Na and O atoms and their atom is also high (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4a</a>). These results indicate that the dominant component for the C═O peak in O 1s spectrum is assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0026_R_d8242329e1235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By comparison, a lower fraction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and the presence of organics (R-O-Na),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0027_R_d8242329e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> considered beneficial for volume buffering and Na<sup>+</sup> transfer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0008_R_d8242329e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Phys. Chem. C ACS Energy Lett.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0027_R_d8242329e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are observed in the cases of MA-SN-FEN and EA-SN-FEC (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4b–e</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a–c</a>, Supporting Information). It was considered that the SEI rich with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> generally shows poor anti-reduction and electron-blocking capability due to the low LUMO energy levels and narrow bandgap of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> even with the presence of NaF (a good electron insulator).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e1273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More discussions on F species origin in the cells with EC-PC are presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a> (Supporting Information). Consequently, the presence of less Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and more organic Na<sup>+</sup>-compounds in the SEI derived from the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes enabled stronger electron-blocking and volume buffering effects, responsible for suppressed SEI growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0005_R_d8242329e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These XPS results are consistent with the low LUMO level of the co-solvated cluster (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1g</a>) and the unique solvation and decomposition of carboxylic esters (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403138-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/06ed0b21-e2a3-4402-8fb9-d2c289d287ca/adfm202403138-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/06ed0b21-e2a3-4402-8fb9-d2c289d287ca/adfm202403138-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/99f64ae8-e1be-48b4-8932-3331f53c8996/adfm202403138-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/06ed0b21-e2a3-4402-8fb9-d2c289d287ca/adfm202403138-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403138-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403138" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Components and morphologies of SEI on the cycled HC anodes with the different electrolytes. a–e) XPS results of the SEI components: the quantified atomic ratios of the elements from survey spectra for SEI formed in EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes with increased sputtering time; and the fitted high-resolution XPS spectra of Na 1s (d) and O 1s with increased sputtering time. f–h) HR-TEM images of the HC electrode surface showing the SEI formation (schematically outlined by white dashed lines) from the electrolytes of EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC, and EA-SN-FEC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To verify the function of carboxylic esters for tuning SEI components, we additionally performed XPS measurements on the HC electrodes cycled in an electrolyte of SN-FEC for comparison. The role of SN tuning SEI comments is ruled out because the presence of a very low overall fraction of nitrogen proves limited decomposition of SN in all SN-containing electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0017_R_d8242329e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0028_R_d8242329e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In view of the difference in Na 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a</a>, Supporting Information), we propose that the differences in decreased Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and increased organics in SEI are highly correlated with the carboxylic ester molecules. Meanwhile, by further comparison, we can see carboxylic esters also contribute to regulating the F content for desirable inorganic NaF as well as organic fluorocarbon (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information) and organic R-O-Na species (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4d,e</a>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0007_R_d8242329e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0025_R_d8242329e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0027_R_d8242329e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> both of which are reportedly beneficial for inhibiting SEI growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0008_R_d8242329e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Phys. Chem. C ACS Energy Lett.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0027_R_d8242329e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4f</a> shows an uneven SEI with a thickness of 8.9–22.2&nbsp;nm in the EC-PC electrolyte. In contrast, a relatively uniform SEI with a thickness of 10.4–15.6&nbsp;nm was formed in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0004">4g,h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). This difference originates from the continuous electrolyte decomposition because of the electron leakage (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) with EC-PC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e1382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the results of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements of HC half cells show a smaller interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub>) in the HC cells with our designed electrolytes after 500 cycles at 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the robust anode interphases (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403138-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202403138-sec-0060-title">2.4 Analysis of the CEI Interphase</h3>
            
            <p>We further investigated the cooperative role of solvation by carboxylic esters and SN in regulating the CEI via studying NVOPF cathodes after 300 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the MD simulations, we learned that carboxylic esters, SN, and FEC cooperatively solvate Na<sup>+</sup>, and thus the interphase formed is expected to be a function of the dominance of particular solvents in the first solvation sheath. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5a,c</a> show a higher N fraction and a lower F fraction in the CEI formed in MA-SN-FEC compared to that in EA-SN-FEC,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0025_R_d8242329e1425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0028_R_d8242329e1425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating that the short-chain carboxylate, MA, with less steric hindrance, facilitates a more pronounced SN decomposition at high voltage. Therefore, the cell with MA-SN-FEC shows slightly higher capacity retention over cycling compared to that with EA-SN-FEC (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0002">2c,d</a>). Nevertheless, the sum of F and N for the CEI respectively in MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC are rather close (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5a</a>), indicating the co-decomposition of SN and FEC synergistically contributes to the desired inorganics of NaF and Na<sub>3</sub>N (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5a–c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22d–f</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24b–d</a>, Supporting Information). The higher fractions of Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaF may impart the CEI with desired mechanical properties, high conductivity, and lower solubility,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0025_R_d8242329e1450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0028_R_d8242329e1450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can lead to inhibited CEI damage and electrolyte decomposition under high voltage. This is reflected by reduced capacity loss upon open-circuit pause as discussed below. In contrast, for the CEI in EC-PC electrolyte, the prevalent inorganic components are Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF, and their fractions increase with the sputtering time (Na 1s spectra; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0025_R_d8242329e1463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, it is reported that at high voltage, the NVOPF cathode with carbonate electrolyte suffers from structural degradation, thus leading to an F-contained CEI in the EC-PC electrolyte (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a,b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0029_R_d8242329e1472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403138-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9c1ba064-9ea2-466b-bd54-ad2da87c9b80/adfm202403138-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9c1ba064-9ea2-466b-bd54-ad2da87c9b80/adfm202403138-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/546f4254-1b3f-4598-941a-d5026e3bae4e/adfm202403138-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9c1ba064-9ea2-466b-bd54-ad2da87c9b80/adfm202403138-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403138-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403138" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Components, morphologies, and dissolution of CEI on the cycled NVOPF cathodes using various electrolytes after 300 cycles. a–c) XPS depth etching results collected from the cycled NVOPF cathodes: the quantified atomic ratios of elements from survey spectra for the CEI formed from MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes with increased sputtering time; and the fitted high-resolution F 1s spectra of the CEI using EC-PC, MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes without sputtering; and N 1s spectra of the CEI with three electrolytes without sputtering. d-f) HR-TEM images for the EC-PC (d), MA-SN-FEC (e), and EA-SN-FEC (f) electrolytes derived CEI, as schematically shown by the white dashed lines. g–i) Evaluation of CEI dissolution based on voltage/capacity loss of the cycled NVOPF cells over open-circuit pauses: the voltage drops of the cells during the open-circuit pause of 75&nbsp;h; the absolute capacity loss and the capacity retention ratio for cells using different electrolytes at each pause with two cells in parallel for confirmation. The capacity loss (<i>Q</i><sub>loss</sub>) and the capacity retention (<i>R</i>) are calculated based on the capacity delivered before and after a pause (e.g., <i>Q</i><sub>loss,45st</sub> = <i>Q</i><sub>discharge,44st</sub> − <i>Q</i><sub>discharge,45th</sub>, and <i>R</i><sub>45st</sub> = <i>Q</i><sub>discharge,45st</sub>/<i>Q</i><sub>discharge,44th</sub>).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After 900 cycles, the NVOPF half cells with the MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes show better interfacial charge transfer kinetics than the cell with EC-PC electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub>: 40&nbsp;Ω vs. 120&nbsp;Ω; further discussed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), as is similar to that with resumed cycling to 1400 times (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25b</a>, Supporting Information). The HR-TEM images further show an uneven and thick CEI formed on the NVOPF electrode surface in EC-PC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5d</a>) as opposed to the uniform and thin CEI formed in MA-SN-FEC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information) and EA-SN-FEC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5f</a>), reflecting suppressed electrolyte consumption and less CEI damage with SN and carboxylate as the co-solvents.</p>
            
            <p>We further evaluated the interphase stability by examining the capacity and voltage loss of a cycled cell paused at the fully charged states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e1551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0004_R_d8242329e1551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, other than the differences in the electrolytes, all other conditions, including the electrode material, sodium metal counter electrode, separator, and the charging state, remained the same in all testing cells. Therefore, the differences in the cell voltage and delivered capacity originate from the electrolyte and the cell interphases after pausing. The NVOPF half cells were first cycled in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V at 1 C and then paused for 25, 50, 75, and 100 h after every 15 cycles. The cells with MA-SN-FEC and EA-SN-FEC electrolytes show a significantly lower loss in capacity and voltage than the ones with EC-PC (two cells were measured in parallel for accuracy, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5g–i</a>), especially for those with long pauses of over 50&nbsp;h. After pausing for 75&nbsp;h, the average capacity loss and voltage drop for the cells using EC-PC are 61.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and ≈0.69&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a</a>, Supporting Information), which is about twice of those using MA-SN-FEC (31.96&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.34&nbsp;V; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27b</a>, Supporting Information) and EA-SN-FEC (26.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.31&nbsp;V; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27c</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the bulk structure of the cycled NVOPF cathodes was maintained over 1400 cycles at 1&nbsp;C, as shown by the XRD pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), supporting that the fast capacity fade of cell with EC-PC electrolyte is mainly due to the interphase degradation. Therefore, the alleviated energy loss in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information) (shown by the colored area) and higher capacity retention for the cells using carboxylic ester-based electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403138-fig-0005">5h</a>) are effective indicators for a stabilized CEI with less Na<sup>+</sup> draining into electrolytes.</p>
            
            <p>The lack of an established interphase regulation principle has hindered the development of high-energy SIBs. Most low-temperature SIB electrolytes have been based on ether solvents with low oxidative stability &lt; 4&nbsp;V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), leading to safety concerns and incompatibility with high-voltage cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e1592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The approach of increasing salt concentration, i.e., high-concentration electrolytes (HCEs) showed success in solving the problem; however, they suffer from high viscosity and poor wettability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e1597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano ACS Nano eScience">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0003_R_d8242329e1597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Dilute electrolytes enable fast charge transfer kinetics but suffer from poor film-forming capability at demanding conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0015_R_d8242329e1603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, localized high-concentration electrolytes (LHCEs) containing “inert” diluents have been developed as a trade-off between rate capability and long-term stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0007_R_d8242329e1608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0013_R_d8242329e1608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Mater. Nat. Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These efforts on regulating the solvation structure with innovation in solvents or additives showed encouraging success.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403138-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403138-bib-0002_R_d8242329e1613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Exploration Adv. Energy Mater.
